      <div style="right:0" class="readable stacked" id="description">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer2003061762931400514">s/t: Bloodcurdling Tales of Horror &amp; the Macabre<br />Lovecraft is "the American writer of the twentieth century most frequently compared with Poe, in the quality of his art ... [and] its thematic preoccupations (the obsessive depiction of psychic disintegration in the face of cosmic horror)," writes Joyce Carol Oates in the <em>New York Review of Books</em>. Del Rey has reprinted Lo</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2003061762931400514">s/t: Bloodcurdling Tales of Horror &amp; the Macabre<br />Lovecraft is "the American writer of the twentieth century most frequently compared with Poe, in the quality of his art ... [and] its thematic preoccupations (the obsessive depiction of psychic disintegration in the face of cosmic horror)," writes Joyce Carol Oates in the <em>New York Review of Books</em>. Del Rey has reprinted Lovecraft's stories in three handsome paperbacks. This first volume collects 16 classic tales, including "The Rats in the Walls," "The Call of Cthulhu," "The Dunwich Horror," and "The Colour Out of Space." Introduction by Robert Bloch. Wraparound cover art by Michael Whelan.</span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1342144?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jan 04, 2016</a>

      <a href="/user/show/83582-bill-kerwin" class="user" title="Bill  Kerwin">Bill  Kerwin</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/36315.The_Best_of_H_P_Lovecraft" title="Paperback 9780345350800" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/83582-bill-kerwin?shelf=weird-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">weird-fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/83582-bill-kerwin?shelf=short-stories" class="actionLinkLite">short-stories</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1342144">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14419482088775151599"><br />I know, I know . . . the diction is unnecessarily latinate and the prose is frequently overwrought, piling up the adjectives like "shambling" and "eldritch" to the point where certain passages are laugh-out-loud funny. <br /><br />And yet . . . Lovecraft has fashioned from the New World's New England a land so very old, a world in touch with realities so alien, that Christianity--albeit peripherally present--is completely irrelevant, and mere sanity--the best one can reasonably hope for--depends upon a few</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText14419482088775151599"><br />I know, I know . . . the diction is unnecessarily latinate and the prose is frequently overwrought, piling up the adjectives like "shambling" and "eldritch" to the point where certain passages are laugh-out-loud funny. <br /><br />And yet . . . Lovecraft has fashioned from the New World's New England a land so very old, a world in touch with realities so alien, that Christianity--albeit peripherally present--is completely irrelevant, and mere sanity--the best one can reasonably hope for--depends upon a few ancient formulae and extraordinary personal luck. The precisely imagined landscape convinces the reader, even though the prose often fails. <br /><br />Lovecraft makes us believe in his world, and his world is genuinely terrifying. <br /><br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="14419482088775151599">...more</a>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown">
    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_24883781">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/24883781" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1251168-ayla-zachary" class="left imgcol" title="Ayla Zachary"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1213882700p2/1251168.jpg" alt="Ayla Zachary" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/24883781?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jun 19, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1251168-ayla-zachary" class="user" title="Ayla Zachary">Ayla Zachary</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/36315.The_Best_of_H_P_Lovecraft" title="Paperback 9780345350800" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer24883781">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8412546810211733505">H. P. Lovecraft is a peculiar writer. His stories are extremely predictable. The first-person narrator, a sober man of reason and science, will halfway through the story start noticing something odd about his surroundings: "It was almost as though [horrifying revelation from the end of the story], but I knew that could not be the case." And then, at the end, when all his reason has been denied, "It was then I knew the terrible truth: [horrifying revelation that we all guessed thirty pages ago]!"</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText8412546810211733505">H. P. Lovecraft is a peculiar writer. His stories are extremely predictable. The first-person narrator, a sober man of reason and science, will halfway through the story start noticing something odd about his surroundings: "It was almost as though [horrifying revelation from the end of the story], but I knew that could not be the case." And then, at the end, when all his reason has been denied, "It was then I knew the terrible truth: [horrifying revelation that we all guessed thirty pages ago]!" He also tends to use the same handful of adjectives over and over again: "ancient," "blasphemous," and "cyclopean" occur far more often than is really necessary.<br /><br />But for all that, there is still some strange magical property to his writing. A personal example: the first time I read Lovecraft, I was visiting my sister on spring break. I wanted something to read before going to bed, so I picked up <em>The Best of H.P.L.</em> and started reading "The Call of Cthulu." Halfway through the story, I was thinking "Wow, this is really kind of hack-y and predictable." I was starting to feel rather afraid, though what of I couldn't tell you. Then, I went to sleep, and had scary effed-up nightmares all night about undersea cities and evil giant monsters. Which, I think, is rather the point of reading H. P. Lovecraft.<br /><br />He's not a terribly good writer, but his stories have a strange way of getting into your subconscious and taking root.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="8412546810211733505">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_850830336">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/850830336" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/2457095-apatt" class="left imgcol" title="Apatt"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1349239948p2/2457095.jpg" alt="Apatt" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/850830336?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Oct 05, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/2457095-apatt" class="user" title="Apatt">Apatt</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/36315.The_Best_of_H_P_Lovecraft" title="Paperback 9780345350800" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/2457095-apatt?shelf=favorites" class="actionLinkLite">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer850830336">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14248440498698085166"><blockquote> <em>“Ph'nglui mglw'nafh Cthulhu R'lyeh wgah'nagl fhtagn”</em> </blockquote>Try saying that backward (or forward, which is equally challenging).<br /><br />H.P. Lovecraft is definitely the granddaddy of “Cosmic Horror” and Weird Fiction. He is often mentioned in science fiction / fantasy / horror related websites and forums, not to mention myriad other kinds of websites. Reading fans raving about his works and seeing the numerous fan arts online make many of us genre fiction enthusiasts want to start getting into his fiction to</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText14248440498698085166"><blockquote>
  <em>“Ph'nglui mglw'nafh Cthulhu R'lyeh wgah'nagl fhtagn”</em>
</blockquote>Try saying that backward (or forward, which is equally challenging).<br /><br />H.P. Lovecraft is definitely the granddaddy of “Cosmic Horror” and Weird Fiction. He is often mentioned in science fiction / fantasy / horror related websites and forums, not to mention myriad other kinds of websites. Reading fans raving about his works and seeing the numerous fan arts online make many of us genre fiction enthusiasts want to start getting into his fiction to see what the fuss is all about. I suspect a lot of first-time readers of Lovecraft are disappointed at what they find. The way he goes about telling his stories is very idiosyncratic, he has a tendency to overwrite and be highly verbose. This can be very disappointing and off-putting if you choose the wrong story to start with and you were expecting a quick thrilling read.<br /><br />This is where the unwieldy titled <em>The Best of H.P. Lovecraft: Bloodcurdling Tales of Horror and the Macabre</em> comes in. This is a “greatest hits” type of anthology which is ideal for the uninitiated and of course fans who want their favorite stories all in one book. It does not include the novellas <a title="At the Mountains of Madness by H.P. Lovecraft" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/32767.At_the_Mountains_of_Madness">At the Mountains of Madness</a> and <a title="The Case of Charles Dexter Ward by H.P. Lovecraft" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/129327.The_Case_of_Charles_Dexter_Ward">The Case of Charles Dexter Ward</a>, which is just as well as these are not so suitable starting places. I think it is better to get used to (and forgive) the author’s verbiage and appreciate the otherwise awesomeness of his stories.<br /><br />A lot of the stories tend to be supernatural / sci-fi hybrids with witches and wizards summoning aliens from another planet or dimension by magic. Here is my quick run-through of the stories in this collection:<br /><br /><em>Introduction by <a title="Robert Bloch" href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/12540.Robert_Bloch">Robert Bloch</a></em> - Best known for <a title="Psycho by Robert Bloch" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/156427.Psycho">Psycho</a>, one of his protégés. A good intro to Lovecraft the man and his fiction. Don’t skip it.<br /><br /><em>The Rats in the Walls</em> - As your very first Lovecraft story his convoluted prose style may take a while to get used to. The climax is spectacular but also a bit of a mess in the narrative department. The story is great though, worth a reread later on.<br /><br /><em>The Picture in the House </em> - The book that drips blood; where the useless protagonist runs away just when things are heating up to a critical point, next time stay at home old chap! Nice, short and atmospheric though.<br /><br /><em>The Outsider </em> - A story of self-discovery. Great twist at the end. Awesome in a most eldritch way.<br /><br /><em>Pickman’s Model</em> - You are in for a treat with this one. Classic Lovecraft, one of his most popular and enduring stories. The colloquial writing style is rather unusual for Lovecraft I think. Possibly his most accessible story and a great starting point for new readers.<br /><br /><em>In the Vault </em> - Break a leg! An amusing and rather inconsequential little story.<br /><br /><em>The Silver Key </em> - Time traveling shenanigan featuring some Lovecraft’s patented awful faux-hillbilly dialog. A Twilight Zone-ish story.<br /><br /><em>The Music of Erich Zann</em> - Featuring a man who is attracted by weird music. Next time just buy a Yoko Ono album. Actually one of HPL’s most popular stories. Doesn’t really do much for me, unfortunately. The bloody protagonist does a runner again just when things are getting interesting.<br /><br /><em>The Call of Cthulhu</em> - The narrative is a little fragmented and the story is rather incohesive, but there is some tremendous world building going on in this story. The creepy atmosphere is very well done and for once the monster actually shows up in all its glory (HPL’s monsters generally prefer to lurk and mess with your head). This story is also often cited as evidence of his racism. According to <a title="Robert Bloch" href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/12540.Robert_Bloch">Robert Bloch</a> Lovecraft did become more mellow and tolerant of foreigners after marriage.<br /><br /><em>The Dunwich Horror </em> - This is what newcomers to Lovecraft are probably looking for. A great, thrilling and creepy tale. That Wilbur Whateley reminds me of Damian in the Omen movies a bit to begin with. He changes later on though (not for the better of course)<br /><br /><em>The Whisperer in Darkness </em> - Gives new meaning to “the Kodak moment”, talk about product placement! A very creepy story featuring weird floating monstrosities and a whispery ET.<br /><br /><em>The Colour Out of Space </em> - This! Ladies and gentlemen. This! Lovecraft’s best story (IMO). For a change, the story is pure sci-fi, no chanting monks, witches, voodoo or Cthulhu. The poor Gardners’ family literal disintegration thanks to a meteor falling on their farm will surely give you the heebie-jeebies.<br /><br /><em>The Haunter of the Dark </em> - Set in Italy. The story of a weird black church. If you spot a copy of the <em> Necronomicon </em> by “Mad Arab” Abdul Alhazred in a church head for the exit immediately.<br /><br /><em>The Thing on the Doorstep </em> - This also! What a great body swap story, much better than Freaky Friday. Featuring the eponymous Thing on the Doorstep whose catchphrase is “Glub!”. Brrrr!<br /><br /><em>The Shadow Over Innsmouth </em> - Oh my Gawd! A blasphemously amazing story of some very fishy folks. Set mainly in the creepiest town ever. Featuring a very cool twist.<br /><br /><em>The Dreams in the Witch-House </em> Featuring a witch, a rat with a man’s face and a sort of hyperspace bypass. The narrative is a little rambling for my taste, but a great story is embedded in there.<br /><br /><em>The Shadow Out of Time </em> - Another story of involuntary body swap. The Great Race aliens are almost benign by HPL’s standard, unauthorized body swap notwithstanding. It is a longish story (70 pages or so), it starts off very fascinating, but Lovecraft goes into his rambling mode in the second half of the story. An example of his overwriting. Still a great story though, one that will stay with you.<br /><br />Due to his verbiage, thin characters and appalling dialogs Lovecraft’s dissenters often dismiss him as a bad writer. If so he is the most excellent bad writer of all time. The thing about his writing is that while some of the stories will have you nodding off while wading through the long winded prose, but once you get to the end of the stories you realize that they are actually quite good. Also when he is on top form, such as in <em>The Colour Out of Space </em> where the narrative is very evocative and the story is just right, he is unbeatable.<br /><br />_______________________________<br /><br /><u>Notes:</u><br /><br />The website <a rel="nofollow" href="http://cthulhuchick.com/">Cthulhuchick</a> has kindly put together a free e-book of the Complete Works of H.P. Lovecraft in several formats. The download link is on the main front page.<br /><br />You can read any and all of Lovecraft's stories online at <a rel="nofollow" href="http://www.dagonbytes.com/thelibrary/lovecraft/index.html">Dagonbytes</a>.<br /><br />Download links for <a rel="nofollow" href="http://unfilmable.blogspot.com/2010/11/call-of-cthulhu-and-other-audio.html">free Lovecraft audiobooks</a>.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="14248440498698085166">...more</a>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_417471310">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/417471310" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/6313408-jonathan" class="left imgcol" title="Jonathan"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1450116936p2/6313408.jpg" alt="Jonathan" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/417471310?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Oct 31, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/6313408-jonathan" class="user" title="Jonathan">Jonathan</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/36315.The_Best_of_H_P_Lovecraft" title="Paperback 9780345350800" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/6313408-jonathan?shelf=personal-favourites" class="actionLinkLite">personal-favourites</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/6313408-jonathan?shelf=classic-literature" class="actionLinkLite">classic-literature</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/6313408-jonathan?shelf=mystery" class="actionLinkLite">mystery</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/6313408-jonathan?shelf=science-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/6313408-jonathan?shelf=fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/6313408-jonathan?shelf=short-stories" class="actionLinkLite">short-stories</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/6313408-jonathan?shelf=fantasy-sci-fi-challenge" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy-sci-fi-challenge</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/6313408-jonathan?shelf=classic-challenge" class="actionLinkLite">classic-challenge</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/6313408-jonathan?shelf=gothic" class="actionLinkLite">gothic</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer417471310">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12081363395466123862"><br />It was only last year that I discovered the joy of short stories thanks to <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/218334679">Anton Chekhov</a> and <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/219224909">Edgar Allan Poe</a> (although it seems longer since time is a "great ball of wibbly wobbly timey wimey stuff"). Since then I have been interested in the great short story authors of all time (as well as writing my own short stories), among whom Lovecraft is often mentioned. So I was very optimistic about this volume of stories when I started to read it - particularly in regards to the 'infamous Cthulhu Mytho</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText12081363395466123862"><br />It was only last year that I discovered the joy of short stories thanks to <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/218334679">Anton Chekhov</a> and <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/219224909">Edgar Allan Poe</a> (although it seems longer since time is a "great ball of wibbly wobbly timey wimey stuff"). Since then I have been interested in the great short story authors of all time (as well as writing my own short stories), among whom Lovecraft is often mentioned. So I was very optimistic about this volume of stories when I started to read it - particularly in regards to the 'infamous Cthulhu Mythos.'<br /><br />This volume opened with a brilliant foreword by Robert Bloch which described why Lovecraft deserves to be among the greats of horror writing and short story writing. When I read his stories I found them to have a similar effervescence and mellifluous beauty to Poe and in some parts the simplicity of Chekhov, yet clearly the work was one hundred percent unique. What Lovecraft has done with his short stories is create a world that focuses on the unknown. His terror is not simple blood and gore shock tactics to scare the audience, it is the horror that requires a deeper level of skill, to creep the audience out by linking the narrators to mysterious and creepy terrors. I found the supernatural and science fiction elements of his horror unique to him as while his language may have been similar to other short story artists the themes were completely unique and specific to his work. Very few other short story writers have written short stories that constantly link while telling different stories.<br /><br />What are the themes of these short stories? Well Lovecraft deals in a variety of themes such as: <br />dreams, mirrors and hence the self, the 'other', the unknown, the danger of cults and occult powers, corpses, tombs, old ruined houses, cannibalism, science vs. religion, the cosmos, monsters vs. men, crawling and slimy creatures, insanity and psychology. <br />It seemed to me that Lovecraft believed not in deities as such or had a personal religion but was more a scientist who believed in the power of the cosmos. His view seems that the universe is strange, chaotic and hence unordered by the power of one supreme being but perhaps there may be old powers that exist in the "black seas of infinity" and will exist after people. Of course while my beliefs are very different it was interesting to note this in his fiction. He seemed to still hold a concept of black and white morality despite there being very much a greyness about his stance on human life and the powers in the universe. Curious...<br /><br />The thing I also loved about the stories was pointed out to me by the foreword. Where most novels and short stories have either an unreliable* or a reliable** narrator Lovecraft wrote stories that had both at once in some strange way. The way he did this was to write first person narratives with characters who had the information but also some kind of flaw to make you doubt where the reliability was or was not. For instance this particularly occurs in the first story in this volume. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span style="display: none" class="spoilerContainer">[Where the narrator appears to describe everything reliably until right at the end we discover that he is in fact narrating from an asylum and he is being classed as insane. However he claims he's not insane. So which viewpoint do we take? <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />I strongly recommend also that anyone interested in Lovecraft check out the essay he wrote called<a rel="nofollow" href="http://gaslight.mtroyal.ab.ca/superhor.htm"> Supernatural horror in literature</a>. It provides several unique insights and perspectives. I also naturally recommend his tales if you're into short stories or gothic horror fiction that meets a kind of supernatural science fiction horror.<br /><br />*That is you're never quite sure whether what they're telling you is all the facts or 'the truth' such as in the case of <a title="Dracula by Bram Stoker" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17245.Dracula">Dracula</a> for instance. <br />**<s>Work it out from my definition of unreliable narrator</s> That is a narrator who you believe to be telling all the facts or at least enough to trust his perspective. Think Nick from <a title="The Great Gatsby by F. Scott Fitzgerald" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4671.The_Great_Gatsby">The Great Gatsby</a>.<!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="12081363395466123862">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer8123214346673069025">Strangely enough, I actually found his prose to be quite approachable before I turned ten. Before that was all RL Stine and after reading all the "cla</span>

  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/289629336" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/7213075-ted" class="left imgcol" title="Ted"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1414699107p2/7213075.jpg" alt="Ted" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/289629336?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Aug 21, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/7213075-ted" class="user" title="Ted">Ted</a>

              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer289629336">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4830816447169354696"><strong>I’ve recently been prompted to add a few words to this review</strong> by a review <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1031264729">by Glenn Russell</a> of a Lovecraft short story called <em>From Beyond</em>. (In his review Glenn provides <a rel="nofollow" href="http://gutenberg.net.au/ebooks06/0600031h.html">this link</a> to a Guttenburg Australia site titled the Collected Stories of Lovecraft.)<br /><br /><img class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1408653808i/10879676.jpg" /><br /><strong>H.P. Lovecraft, 1934, age 43. ~3 years until he passed on to … what?</strong><br /><br />These are great horror stories dating back close to a century now, from one of the stranger American fiction writers. The fictional worlds that Lovecraft created are located in tem</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText4830816447169354696"><strong>I’ve recently been prompted to add a few words to this review</strong> by a review <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1031264729">by Glenn Russell</a> of a Lovecraft short story called <em>From Beyond</em>. (In his review Glenn provides <a rel="nofollow" href="http://gutenberg.net.au/ebooks06/0600031h.html">this link</a> to a Guttenburg Australia site titled the Collected Stories of Lovecraft.)<br /><br /><img class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1408653808i/10879676.jpg" /><br /><strong>H.P. Lovecraft, 1934, age 43. ~3 years until he passed on to … what?</strong><br /><br />These are great horror stories dating back close to a century now, from one of the stranger American fiction writers. The fictional worlds that Lovecraft created are located in temporally shifting realms which intersect with everyday reality in usually horrific ways, inhabited by ancient creatures having no relation to the life forms familiar to us. The stories generally involve an interaction between the “other” (these worlds and creatures), and a human being who has somehow come into contact with “the other”. (The contact often happens through some type of portal, likely in an old abandoned house or castle, perhaps simply in an outdoors location miles from normal human traffic.) The results of such contacts are always pretty unfortunate for the human, resulting in death or, just as frequently, insanity.<br /><br />The “other” that Lovecraft created is often referred to as a “mythos”, specifically as the “Cthulhu Mythos” (from a series of stories which refer to one of these ancient beings by that name). <br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span style="display: none" class="spoilerContainer">[To my knowledge, these are the only stories in the horror literary genre that have ever been made into successful games (the Arkham Horrorboard game, the Call of Cthulhu role playing game). <br /><br />Other literary works on this rather small shelf (sources for games) are mostly fantasy and science fiction tales. Such “game source” stories that I’m familiar with (with associated games) include:<br /><br />Frank Herbert’s Dune (same name); Robert Heinlein’s Starship Troopers (same name); Joe Haldeman’s The Forever War (same name); Anne McCaffrey’s Dragonriders of Pern series (same name); Tolkein’s Lord of the Ring’s trilogy (several games); Marion Zimmer Bradley’s Darkover series (same name); Isaac Asimov’s Foundation trilogy (on which the boardgame Star Traders was loosely based); the Buck Rogers pulp magazine/comic strip series (same name); and One Thousand and One Nights (which served as inspiration for an offbeat game called Tales of the Arabian Nights). <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />If you enjoy horror stories, you are certainly familiar already with Lovecraft. <strong>
  <em>No?!?</em>
</strong> Then surely you <em>must</em> get on board.<br /><br />Lovecraft’s writing style is definitely dated, not too different from Poe’s to an untrained ear like mine. But he really is the preeminent horror writer of the early twentieth century. See his Wiki article <a rel="nofollow" href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/H._P._Lovecraft" target="_blank">http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/H._P._Lo...</a> and his biblio article <a rel="nofollow" href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/H._P._Lovecraft_bibliography" target="_blank">http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/H._P._Lo...</a> which gives a good idea of the range of writing that this man did, without ever really being able to support himself from it.<br /><br />One final comment, on the story <em>From Beyond</em>. It’s quite an impressive tale looking at the horror fiction aspect of <em>an idea</em> which has been taken up by many philosophers, particularly Kant, and (more recently) evolutionists. That is, that we humans are prisoners of our senses. Not only are we locked <em>into</em> a prison which makes us interpret the world through the common sensory abilities which we are all born with; but (as Lovecraft points out) we are, at least possibly, <em>locked out</em> of perceiving things in reality of which we have no idea ... <em>perhaps to our good fortune?</em>.<br /><br /><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="4830816447169354696">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer14796589228439293031">Beautifully written horror that many imitate (ahem, Stephen King) but few can pull off. The real horror of Lovecraft isn't the scariness of the monsters or the gore, but concept that we are pointless blips of dust on the gaping maw of a chaotic, ageless, indifferent universe that constantly destroys itself for no reason at all. Each story reminds you of how puny and ignorant you are but that's a good thing because every character finds out a little too much and goes crazy, gets eaten, sacrificed</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText14796589228439293031">Beautifully written horror that many imitate (ahem, Stephen King) but few can pull off. The real horror of Lovecraft isn't the scariness of the monsters or the gore, but concept that we are pointless blips of dust on the gaping maw of a chaotic, ageless, indifferent universe that constantly destroys itself for no reason at all. Each story reminds you of how puny and ignorant you are but that's a good thing because every character finds out a little too much and goes crazy, gets eaten, sacrificed, has his face ripped off, teleported to another dimension or wakes up as a rotting corpse. Good stuff!</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer12244720694788052497">Well, I don't watch a lot of horror films, but now I see where they all rip off everything from: Lovecraft!<br /><br />I didn't read EVERY one of these stories, they got a bit repetitive after a while, but the stories were chilling and seeing how influential the storytelling is on modern horror is really interesting. Fascinating how much suspense can be put into a 10 page story. <br /><br />Yes, I had nightmares of tentacle-things after I finished. Don't make fun of me.</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_16092832">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/16092832" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/903390-werner" class="left imgcol" title="Werner"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1216337931p2/903390.jpg" alt="Werner" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/16092832?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">May 26, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/903390-werner" class="user" title="Werner">Werner</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/36315.The_Best_of_H_P_Lovecraft" title="Paperback 9780345350800" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Fans of "horror" and of horrific science fiction</span></div>

        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/903390-werner?shelf=science-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/903390-werner?shelf=short-stories" class="actionLinkLite">short-stories</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer16092832">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5264246853339588838">Not well-appreciated in his own time, reclusive and eccentric New England writer Howard Phillips Lovecraft became a household word in the world of weird fiction after his death. His prose style was greatly influenced by Poe, and like Poe, he preferred natural causes for his horror ("supernatural," in one of the alternate titles listed above for this collection, means "uncanny" or "unearthly," not supernatural per se). While his genre was science fiction, he was wholly outside the optimistic and</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText5264246853339588838">Not well-appreciated in his own time, reclusive and eccentric New England writer Howard Phillips Lovecraft became a household word in the world of weird fiction after his death. His prose style was greatly influenced by Poe, and like Poe, he preferred natural causes for his horror ("supernatural," in one of the alternate titles listed above for this collection, means "uncanny" or "unearthly," not supernatural per se). While his genre was science fiction, he was wholly outside the optimistic and technophilic hard SF tradition that dominated the genre pulps of his time, publishing instead mostly in venues like <em>Weird Tales</em>. Much of his work is based on what has come to be called his "Cthulhu Mythos" (a term coined by August Derleth): a vision of malevolent alien races who supposedly ruled the primeval Earth, and whose remnants are still very dangerous. A number of the stories here are part of this body of work (which is often internally inconsistent in details from story to story), and some others foreshadow aspects of it. Several of these are set in his imaginary town of Arkham, MA, seat of Miskatonic Univ. --an interesting place to visit, but not one you'd want to live in. :-) (And nearby Innsmouth isn't even a place you'd want to visit!)<br /><br />Unlike Poe, Lovecraft's horror is existential rather than situational; an atheist and an uncritical Darwinist (in "The Rats in the Walls," for instance, investigators probing below the sub-basement of Exham Priory find numerous bones of "Piltdown Men" --some trick, since the species was a hoax!), he regarded the universe as a bleak, meaningless place hostile to humanity, and presents his "Great Old Ones" or "Elder Gods" as the embodiment of that hostility. Awareness of their existence is treated as a knowledge that forever annihilates any conception of meaning or rationality in the universe for those unfortunate enough to stumble on them. For Christians, of course, versed in the concept of Satan, the idea that the universe contains super-powerful and evil entities hostile to mankind evokes no surprise, or fear --but readers with a worldview like that of early 20th-century bourgeois materialism probably would find it soul-shattering. Lovecraft fans fall into two groups: those who find this perspective the best part of his writing and eat it up with a spoon, and those (including myself, and apparently Derleth) who don't, and who may even consider it a defect. But his originality, his story-telling ability, his mastery of style and plot, and powerful evocation of atmosphere make his work highly readable and fascinating despite this.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="5264246853339588838">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <a href="/user/show/531153-jonathan" class="left imgcol" title="Jonathan"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1436116454p2/531153.jpg" alt="Jonathan" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1427457858?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Nov 03, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/531153-jonathan" class="user" title="Jonathan">Jonathan</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1427457858">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9801981823422656506">Not having read him before, I was surprised to find these rather enjoyable. Not remotely disturbing or frightening to the modern mind, of course, but it is interesting to see the fears engendered by the scientific revolutions at the start of the 20thc. Also interesting to see how much of contemporary sci-fi/horror is simply a regurgitation of his ideas. </span>

          </span>

      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1454640">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1454640" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/84023-j-g-keely" class="left imgcol" title="J.G. Keely"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1274333636p2/84023.jpg" alt="J.G. Keely" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1454640?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">May 03, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/84023-j-g-keely" class="user" title="J.G. Keely">J.G. Keely</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/84023-j-g-keely?shelf=science-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/84023-j-g-keely?shelf=fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/84023-j-g-keely?shelf=short-story" class="actionLinkLite">short-story</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/84023-j-g-keely?shelf=horror" class="actionLinkLite">horror</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/84023-j-g-keely?shelf=novel" class="actionLinkLite">novel</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/84023-j-g-keely?shelf=pulp" class="actionLinkLite">pulp</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/84023-j-g-keely?shelf=reviewed" class="actionLinkLite">reviewed</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/84023-j-g-keely?shelf=gothic" class="actionLinkLite">gothic</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/84023-j-g-keely?shelf=america" class="actionLinkLite">america</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/84023-j-g-keely?shelf=supernatural-horror" class="actionLinkLite">supernatural-horror</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1454640">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8954291972863595891"><blockquote> <em>"There are my 'Poe' pieces and my 'Dunsany pieces' – but alas – where are any Lovecraft pieces?"<br /><br />-H.P. Lovecraft, 1929</em> </blockquote><br />What really makes Lovecraft interesting is the degree to which he was a student of the Horror genre. As his influential essay <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/577015131">Supernatural Horror in Literature</a> shows, Lovecraft was a voracious reader who went far afield in his search for interesting Horror authors. If Lovecraft hadn't been such an odd recluse, and instead pursued an academic career, we might not have had to wait</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText8954291972863595891"><blockquote>
  <em>"There are my 'Poe' pieces and my 'Dunsany pieces' – but alas – where are any Lovecraft pieces?"<br /><br />-H.P. Lovecraft, 1929</em>
</blockquote><br />What really makes Lovecraft interesting is the degree to which he was a student of the Horror genre. As his influential essay <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/577015131">Supernatural Horror in Literature</a> shows, Lovecraft was a voracious reader who went far afield in his search for interesting Horror authors. If Lovecraft hadn't been such an odd recluse, and instead pursued an academic career, we might not have had to wait a century for scholar S.T. Joshi to drag the genre into the sphere of literary criticism.<br /><br />Due to his vast knowledge, Lovecraft was able to pick through influences and styles when he wrote his stories, but instead of synthesizing all of those disparate inspirations into a new vision of his own, Lovecraft was more likely to work in bits and pieces, creating recognizable, sometimes formulaic story types in which we can easily trace the ideas he drew from Dunsany, Blackwood, Hodgson, Chambers, or Bierce.<br /><br />Beyond that, his style was not always engaging, relying as he did on rather purple prose and extended explanations of his characters' innermost thoughts, instead of letting the actions and subtle cues speak for themselves. As such, his stories tended to lack the power and poetry of the great Horror authors who influenced him, but Lovecraft was such a prolific author, and so invested in his genre on a conceptual level that he did create a number of classics.<br /><br />He also had a considerable influence on other writers through the vast correspondence which he kept up throughout his whole life with lasting, notable authors such as R.E. Howard, Fritz Leiber, Derleth, Clarke Ashton Smith, and numerous others, not only introducing them to the many ideas and authors Lovecraft had collected, but opening himself up to the thoughts and experiences of all those young, up-and-coming authors.<br /><br />Then there is the lasting effect of the 'Mythos' (sometimes called the 'Cthulhu Mythos'), that interconnected set of ideas and approaches that became a sort of 'shared world' for other authors to explore--whit are still being explored in an unabated string of short-story collections published every year despite the fact that all the stories in them tend to be terrible. But the Mythos was not quite Lovecraft's original invention, it was instead an attempt to take the worlds of the previous great Horror authors and combine them into one grand setting.<br /><br />Probably the most unique aspect of Lovecraft's work was his combination of the chilling, aloof alienness of Dunsany's elves with the otherworldly, interdimensional terrors explored by Hodgson to produce that characteristic 'Cosmic Horror' which, while not invented by Lovecraft, was brought to a higher lustre in his works.<br /><br />Though the Lovecraft stories that I find most interesting are not his 'Poe pieces', his straight Horror works--which should not be surprising, since I'm not especially fond of Poe--but his Dunsany-inspired Fantasies, such as <em>The Silver Key</em> and several other entries in his <em>Dream Cycle</em>, which tend to be rather less formulaic recreations of the styles and forms of earlier authors, than explorations of the mind, and of possibility. Beyond that, Lovecraft's very deliberate, thoughtful style seems to work better in a world of waking dreams than one of world-hopping adventure and monster attacks.<br /><br />However, in the end, I would suggest that the most lasting effect of Lovecraft's work was born in his intense dislike of seafood, which gave his monsters and beasties their shapes, and proved a much more effective choice than Hodgson's odd distaste for pig. Indeed, Lovecraft's disgust must surely rank among the most influential gustatory preferences in the history of literature.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="8954291972863595891">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer125287388355583953">I love these classic horror stories. Lovecraft was a very odd man, and that oddness shines through in his work. In other words, he battled a lot of issues such as immigrants "invading" his territory, and he pretty much lacked social skills on all levels. I learned his bio after reading the tales in this book, and that information illuminated many aspects of his stories such as race, gender, madness, etc. While many tales are reminiscent of Poe and Hawthorne, many are pure Lovecraft. I like all t</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText125287388355583953">I love these classic horror stories. Lovecraft was a very odd man, and that oddness shines through in his work. In other words, he battled a lot of issues such as immigrants "invading" his territory, and he pretty much lacked social skills on all levels. I learned his bio after reading the tales in this book, and that information illuminated many aspects of his stories such as race, gender, madness, etc. While many tales are reminiscent of Poe and Hawthorne, many are pure Lovecraft. I like all the works in this book, but I really like "The Outsider" because it is very different from the rest and "The Whisperer in the Darkness" because the building tension to a frenzied level reminds me of the classic 1950s film "Invasion of the Body Snatchers" - even though Lovecraft wrote his tale about 20 years earlier. I'm trying to verify if the movie adaptation is really happening. Hope so!</span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/13039293?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jan 21, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/761281-mike" class="user" title="Mike">Mike</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/36315.The_Best_of_H_P_Lovecraft" title="Paperback 9780345350800" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/761281-mike?shelf=fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/761281-mike?shelf=horror" class="actionLinkLite">horror</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer13039293">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6282731857968578586">Lovecraft is one of the writers people tend to either obsess about or dismiss without a second glance. His writings vary tremendously, in terms of quality. Some of it is really horrible, actually, with absurdly "purple" prose and overwrought hysteria. But there are also really incredible stories among his work too, which conjure up fascinating mythology and alien races, as well leaving utterly disturbing images in the reader's imagination. "The Dreams in the Witch House" is an example of this se</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText6282731857968578586">Lovecraft is one of the writers people tend to either obsess about or dismiss without a second glance. His writings vary tremendously, in terms of quality. Some of it is really horrible, actually, with absurdly "purple" prose and overwrought hysteria. But there are also really incredible stories among his work too, which conjure up fascinating mythology and alien races, as well leaving utterly disturbing images in the reader's imagination. "The Dreams in the Witch House" is an example of this second set of stories, which stay with you long after the reading. <br /><br />Lovecraft's horror usually involves pseudo-scientific phenomena, such as travel between dimensions or across time, and aliens, hybrids, and mutants rather than supernatural creatures. Often "supernatural" phenomena are reimagined as the products of alien science, as in "The dreams in the witch house." But the real horror comes from Lovecraft's vision of a world completely hostile or at best indifferent to mankind, where mankind's aspirations and hopes are as significant as the dreams of a cow in line for the abattoir. <br /><br />I should note that even this "best of" collection has a couple of stinkers, though.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="6282731857968578586">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_74277892">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/74277892" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/104823-kate" class="left imgcol" title="Kate"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1369142716p2/104823.jpg" alt="Kate" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/74277892?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Oct 25, 2009</a>

      <a href="/user/show/104823-kate" class="user" title="Kate">Kate</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/36315.The_Best_of_H_P_Lovecraft" title="Paperback 9780345350800" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/104823-kate?shelf=horror" class="actionLinkLite">horror</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/104823-kate?shelf=age-adult" class="actionLinkLite">age-adult</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/104823-kate?shelf=classics" class="actionLinkLite">classics</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/104823-kate?shelf=sci-fi" class="actionLinkLite">sci-fi</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/104823-kate?shelf=2009" class="actionLinkLite">2009</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer74277892">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1326360025266098280">It's hard to believe that I went so long without having read H.P. Lovecraft, considering that I love horror and most of my favorite horror authors are influenced by his work. For some reason I thought his stories would be hard to connect with - probably because of the whole "Cthulhu" / made up word thing. I found three of his short story collections at the library and chose to begin with this one largely because it contained the story "The Call of Cthulhu."<br /><br />The first few short stories reminded me</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText1326360025266098280">It's hard to believe that I went so long without having read H.P. Lovecraft, considering that I love horror and most of my favorite horror authors are influenced by his work. For some reason I thought his stories would be hard to connect with - probably because of the whole "Cthulhu" / made up word thing. I found three of his short story collections at the library and chose to begin with this one largely because it contained the story "The Call of Cthulhu."<br /><br />The first few short stories reminded me a lot of Edgar Allan Poe, in particular "The Picture in the House," "The Outsider," and "In the Vault" : they were short, had a lot of gothic atmosphere, and usually involved some ironic twist at the end. The other early short stories were strange and only later did I connect them to the "Cthulhu Mythos" that dominates the other half the short stories in this collection. Apparently one of the earlier stories, "The Silver Key," is the start of Lovecraft's "Dream Cycle," and I immediately thought of Joe Hill's <br /><a title="Locke &amp; Key, Vol. 1 Welcome to Lovecraft (Hardcover) by Joe Hill" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/3217221.Locke___Key__Vol__1_Welcome_to_Lovecraft__Hardcover_">Locke &amp; Key, Vol. 1 Welcome to Lovecraft</a>.<br /><br />The Cthulhu stories are more science fiction or speculative fiction, and though not all of them feature that monster Cthulhu, they all involve races of alien creatures that lie somewhat dormant deep inside the earth or in the ocean or in the hills of Vermont, creatures which often inspire small cults of worship in strange places. Often there are stones with "weird geometry" covered in unknown heiroglyphs, and mysterious texts like the Necromicon and others that have instructions as to how to release the aliens from their slumber. The aliens are usually described as being of some amphibian or squid-like nature, with tentacles and green skin, which sounds completely ridiculous, but the way they are discovered in the story somehow makes it seem almost possible. Usually the main character is driven mad by the discovery of these secrets.<br /><br />This was definitely a good book to read around Halloween. I was also a bit disturbed by the geographical proximity of these stories to where I live: Lovecraft grew up in Providence, RI, but he indicates in one story a place past Fitchburg, Ayer, and Gardner, all places I've been and which are about a 20 minutes' drive from my house. I thought the "ironic twist" of some of the stories was a bit weak and obvious, but overall the bizarreness of these alien races lurking in the corners of our own society was what makes these stories memorable.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="1326360025266098280">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_106917089">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/106917089" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/874192-rick" class="left imgcol" title="Rick"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1262403463p2/874192.jpg" alt="Rick" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <a href="/user/show/874192-rick" class="user" title="Rick">Rick</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/874192-rick?shelf=anthologies" class="actionLinkLite">anthologies</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/874192-rick?shelf=xenobiology" class="actionLinkLite">xenobiology</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/874192-rick?shelf=dark-fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">dark-fantasy</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/874192-rick?shelf=lovecraft" class="actionLinkLite">lovecraft</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/874192-rick?shelf=pulp-era-reprints" class="actionLinkLite">pulp-era-reprints</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer106917089">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11866584920593588917">Well, there are certainly some of his best stuff in this collection but these are not necessarily the selections I would have chosen. That being said, this is an excellent anthology of some of the creepiest stuff from one of the masters of horror and who <a title="Stephen King" href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/3389.Stephen_King">Stephen King</a> noted as "yet to be surpassed as the Twentieth Century's greatest practitioner of the classic horror tale." Lovecraft himself stated in the opening of his long essay, <a title="Supernatural Horror in Literature by H.P. Lovecraft" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/666634.Supernatural_Horror_in_Literature">Supernatural Horror in Literature</a> (sadly not included in this par</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText11866584920593588917">Well, there are certainly some of his best stuff in this collection but these are not necessarily the selections I would have chosen. That being said, this is an excellent anthology of some of the creepiest stuff from one of the masters of horror and who <a title="Stephen King" href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/3389.Stephen_King">Stephen King</a> noted as "yet to be surpassed as the Twentieth Century's greatest practitioner of the classic horror tale." Lovecraft himself stated in the opening of his long essay, <a title="Supernatural Horror in Literature by H.P. Lovecraft" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/666634.Supernatural_Horror_in_Literature">Supernatural Horror in Literature</a> (sadly not included in this particular collection), that "The oldest and strongest emotion of mankind is fear, and the oldest and strongest kind of fear is fear of the unknown." I do think the is some room for argument, but I do tend to agree. So this collection might be seen as a kind of offering of appeasement to the elder gods of fear and the unknown or perhaps as a call to arms against the rising xenophobic dread of things from beyond our abilities to comprehend. In either case these stories are prime examples of just how much influence Lovecraft has had on the supernatural as portrayed in fiction, film, television and comic books in the decades since he was published. Discounting his racism, certainly more present in his personal letters than in his fiction, it is irrefutable that Lovecraft's approach and themes revolutionized how consumers and purveyors of dark fantasy viewed the landscape of horror. </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1288615336">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1288615336" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/12862594-luiz" class="left imgcol" title="Luiz"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1347754190p2/12862594.jpg" alt="Luiz" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1288615336?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jul 16, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/12862594-luiz" class="user" title="Luiz">Luiz</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/36315.The_Best_of_H_P_Lovecraft" title="Paperback 9780345350800" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/12862594-luiz?shelf=fiction" class="actionLinkLite">fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/12862594-luiz?shelf=audiobook" class="actionLinkLite">audiobook</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/12862594-luiz?shelf=english" class="actionLinkLite">english</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/12862594-luiz?shelf=short-stories" class="actionLinkLite">short-stories</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1288615336">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1919610819947386413">Excellent collection of the master of horror! I had read this already back in high school, but I didn’t remember much of it. I decided that since there are so many references to Lovecraft’s work all over the place (books, movies, games, comics, etc…), I should give it a re-read. One of the things I do remember was that it was scary. It’s not easy for a book to be scary - movies can easily make you jump, but without the benefit of graphic scenes and loud noises, a book has to do a lot more work t</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText1919610819947386413">Excellent collection of the master of horror! I had read this already back in high school, but I didn’t remember much of it. I decided that since there are so many references to Lovecraft’s work all over the place (books, movies, games, comics, etc…), I should give it a re-read. One of the things I do remember was that it was scary. It’s not easy for a book to be scary - movies can easily make you jump, but without the benefit of graphic scenes and loud noises, a book has to do a lot more work to get you scared - and Lovecraft manages just that. <br /><br />I was surprised by the variety of stories in this collection. A lot of the stories (and none of the earlier ones) stories have nothing to do with the Cthulhu mythos, and are your typical horror stories - an anecdote with a horror twist at the end. But it’s when he gets to the Cthulhu mythos that he really shows his mastery. Some of the stories start as a mystery or adventure story, salted heavily with allusions to hidden things, great mysteries, and “unspeakable horrors”. Despite the exaggerated foreshadowing, Lovecraft does construct a good story, with interesting plot devices (at one point, doom is predicted not by a person having a vision, but by a *statistical analysis* of dreams and visions over a period!), and cliffhangers. His stories sometime link in subtle ways too - you will recognize things in one story that got mentioned in another that in principle had nothing to do with it (they are not sequels), so you slowly get the feeling of a larger world with a lot going on underneath. Another things that surprised me is that this is very much a sci-fi book - the monsters are usually characterized as aliens, be it from other planets or from other “dimensions”. There is a lot of space travel, travel through the folding of space, and the importance of physics and mathematics to the stories. There are even mentions of Einstein, relativity, quantum physics !<br /><br />The major downside of the book is how melodramatic his prose is, specially his use of adjectives. Halfway through the book, it starts getting repetitive, as there are only so many ways to preface the word horror: "indescribable horror”, “Cyclopean horror”, "realms of unfathomable horror and inconceivable abnormality”, etc…<br /><br />There are also an immeasurable number of incongruences to his plots and plot devices. For example, in a few of his stories, there are all-powerful antagonists, ancient beings who have mastered all sorts of dark arts and can destroy all of humanity at a whim, and yet are foiled by dogs (“The Dunwich Horror”, “The Whisperer in Darkness”). Another example is how his characters are always getting horrified by architecture, geometry and language - really, how bad can it be? It makes it difficult to relate sometimes. When someone says how they were paralyzed with fear because of the horrible architecture of the place, it’s not easy to feel sympathetic. A passage says: "My head was aching, and I had a singular feeling that someone else was trying to get possession of my thoughts” You know that feeling that someone is trying to get possession of your thoughts? Yeah, me neither.<br /><br />In the end, if you do get past all the melodrama, you get treated to a vast mythos, with creatures and legends that pop up all over the place (“ah, so this is where that comes from!”), and that have laid important foundations to the genre of horror and sic-fi.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="1919610819947386413">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer65912039">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer954444737457904400">I started the "Best of H.P. Lovecraft" in the middle of the summer, quit for a few months, and just picked it up again. He really is fantastic. He made the modest claim once that "There are my 'Poe' pieces and my 'Dunsany pieces' – but alas – where are my Lovecraft pieces?" While it is true that some of his stories (The Rats in the Walls, The Picture in the House, The Outsider, Pickman's Model, In the Vault, and The Music of Erich Zann) are heavily reminiscent of Poe's works, in my opinion they</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText954444737457904400">I started the "Best of H.P. Lovecraft" in the middle of the summer, quit for a few months, and just picked it up again. He really is fantastic. He made the modest claim once that "There are my 'Poe' pieces and my 'Dunsany pieces' – but alas – where are my Lovecraft pieces?" While it is true that some of his stories (The Rats in the Walls, The Picture in the House, The Outsider, Pickman's Model, In the Vault, and The Music of Erich Zann) are heavily reminiscent of Poe's works, in my opinion they all achieve what Poe was trying to much better than Poe ever did. These are the first stories in the volume, and they were all I had finished by the time I set the book aside this summer. The next set of works, however, seems to me (though I've never read any Dunsany, which they might be derivative of) much more original and much more affecting.<br /><br />This second half of the collection, including The Call of Cthulhu, The Dunwich Horror, The Whisperer in Darkness ( which is perhaps the clearest and broadest articulation of these stories' main premise), The Haunter of the Dark, The Thing on the Doorstep, The Shadow Over Innsmouth, and The Dreams in the Witch-House, all center on one large and flexible set of ideas that create what is referred to as the Cthulhu Mythos. This main thesis is that there are beings in the universe that have properties completely unknown to us, e.g. existence in dimensions beyond the ones we are constrained to, immortality or something appearing like it, rapid travel across the fabric of space-time, etc. The premise of each of the stories, then, is contact between these entities and humanity, and the way they are integrally tied with hidden mystic and magical cults that have been around throughout the history of humanity and now reemerge.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="954444737457904400">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_384206462">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/384206462" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/4856668-dan-phillips" class="left imgcol" title="Dan Phillips"><img src="http://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" alt="Dan Phillips" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/384206462?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Sep 17, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/4856668-dan-phillips" class="user" title="Dan Phillips">Dan Phillips</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/4856668-dan-phillips?shelf=2012" class="actionLinkLite">2012</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer384206462">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer994723136974225300">In high school, I went through a horror phase, and so I was deeply into Lovecraft. A recent viewing of a documentary inspired me to dig up this old "greatest hits" I had lying around. (I used to have hardcover Arkham editions of 'At the Mountains of Madness' and 'The Dunwich Horror and Other Stories,' but I must have sold them years ago...)<br /><br />Initially, I was impressed all over again with the foreboding in each story -- the idea that there are forces far older than humankind slumbering underground</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText994723136974225300">In high school, I went through a horror phase, and so I was deeply into Lovecraft. A recent viewing of a documentary inspired me to dig up this old "greatest hits" I had lying around. (I used to have hardcover Arkham editions of 'At the Mountains of Madness' and 'The Dunwich Horror and Other Stories,' but I must have sold them years ago...)<br /><br />Initially, I was impressed all over again with the foreboding in each story -- the idea that there are forces far older than humankind slumbering underground or under sea, just waiting to reclaim our feebly held patches of Earth. (If you're familiar with Lovecraft, you'll want to throw in an "etc." here...) "Pickman's Model" and "The Music of Erich Zann" are both effective through their brevity, suggesting rather than showing the horror, allowing the reader to exercise imagination in a way that had me feeling like a kid again (honestly). "The Dunwich Horror" and especially "The Call of Cthulhu" are even better for taking a somewhat opposite, detail-intensive attack. In these longer tales, professors, doctors, journalists and detectives weave together third-hand recollections, newspaper accounts, and academic findings. The results are oddly compelling proofs of the "Old Ones" in our midst, lurking on the fringes of our understanding / sanity.<br /><br />The trouble is that once you understand what Lovecraft is driving at, some of the steam goes out of the experience. Think of each story as a kind of mystery, only instead of asking who committed the murder, the question is whether the freaky stuff Our Hero is uncovering through his travels and/or research is true. No matter how convoluted the means by which we arrive there, no matter how Twilight-Zone twisty its delivery, the answer is always "yes." And with all due respect to the craftsmanship involved in this kind of story-telling, you really don't need (or I guess I really don't need) 375 dense pages of it. I know there are slimmer volumes of Lovecraft out there, and I would definitely recommend one of those over this. Still, I rate this four stars just for the experience of reading Lovecraft in general.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="994723136974225300">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer4469086017869764443">I agree. I may have finally finished reading all of the stories in this, but I'm not really sure. I read it over so many years. 2-3 stories at a time</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText4469086017869764443">I agree. I may have finally finished reading all of the stories in this, but I'm not really sure. I read it over so many years. 2-3 stories at a time (per year?) is the right rate if Lovecraft I think. So much of the impact of the stories rests on the other-worldly things being unexpected. They would benefit from some misdirection. As a result I often found myself appreciating the stories more than enjoying them. I would think "wow, if I were that character, that would be really really mind-blowingly scary. Thankfully I'm not that character." Maybe if the stories were more contemporary they would be more effective, because the everything-normal -so-far parts would be more familiar.</span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/237632858?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Dec 09, 2011</a>

      <a href="/user/show/4841374-earl-grey-tea" class="user" title="Earl Grey Tea">Earl Grey Tea</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/36315.The_Best_of_H_P_Lovecraft" title="Paperback 9780345350800" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/4841374-earl-grey-tea?shelf=science-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/4841374-earl-grey-tea?shelf=short-stories" class="actionLinkLite">short-stories</a>,
