        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer237632858">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1132969873670894498">Hooray for xenophobia! Lovecraft's prejudice is not just against those nasty extraterrestrial monsters that drive average human's psyche to the point of oblivion; it also includes oversea immigrants that are from a non-Anglo background. It is interesting to see the mentality of a person from almost a hundred years ago, but it is a bit disappointing that this discrimination in the writing is not just the character reflecting the times, but also is reflecting the author's own mentality.<br /><br />Beyond the</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText1132969873670894498">Hooray for xenophobia! Lovecraft's prejudice is not just against those nasty extraterrestrial monsters that drive average human's psyche to the point of oblivion; it also includes oversea immigrants that are from a non-Anglo background. It is interesting to see the mentality of a person from almost a hundred years ago, but it is a bit disappointing that this discrimination in the writing is not just the character reflecting the times, but also is reflecting the author's own mentality.<br /><br />Beyond the bigotry and lack of political correctness, it is good to finally see exactly what exactly is the inspiration for the Arkham Horror board game that I used to play into the wee hours of the night during college. Hats off to Richard Launius for developing Lovecraft's world into a board game assessable to the modern day geek.<br /><br />As for the text itself, this is no light read. Be prepared to sit down and really focus to decently understand what is going on. Drowsy commutes home on the subway Friday evening are probably not the best place to read this book. The writing style could probably be compared to Edgar Allen Poe; much of the story seems a bit obtuse to me and many of the endings seemed quite anticlimactic. For all of the stories in this collection, Lovecraft used the same style of writing for almost all of the stories. Basically, the (male) narrator starts the story exclaiming that his wits are gone and then proceeds to start from the beginning of the story. For me, this takes the fear out of the story since I know that the protagonist will be alive by the end of the story.<br /><br />Despite that, there were a few scenes here and there that frightened me to an extent. One notably was from "The Shadow of Innsmouth" which is akin to most of modern day horror of some unknown entity outside your room trying to enter. Another story that I really enjoyed was "The Colour Out of Space." After a tiny bit of research, it turns out to be not only one of his most popular works, but also Lovercraft's favourite (a small tribute to H.P.'s anglophilia) stories. I am not sure if there was an "evolution" or "development" in writing styles from his time period, but as a twenty first century reader, I could really get into this story and flow of the writing made it easy to follow.<br /><br />Overall, while there were some interesting ideas, concepts and story lines, this book felt more like something I would have to read for an English Literature class and not something I would sit down and just enjoy.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="1132969873670894498">...more</a>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_315345287">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/315345287" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/6903743-jonathan-briggs" class="left imgcol" title="Jonathan Briggs"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1419812724p2/6903743.jpg" alt="Jonathan Briggs" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/315345287?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Apr 19, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/6903743-jonathan-briggs" class="user" title="Jonathan Briggs">Jonathan Briggs</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/36315.The_Best_of_H_P_Lovecraft" title="Paperback 9780345350800" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer315345287">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2439163609225968322">Reading this collection straight through isn't recommended as that would spotlight H.P. Lovecraft's rather severe limitations as a writer. His dialogue is atrocious. His descriptions are repetitive (except when he resorts to the copout of the horror so horrible it cannot be described). His heroes are generally wussies prone to faint dead away at the hint of an oozing tentacle. And I'm not even going to get into his virulent racism and unfortunate choice in cat names. But taken in smaller doses,</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2439163609225968322">Reading this collection straight through isn't recommended as that would spotlight H.P. Lovecraft's rather severe limitations as a writer. His dialogue is atrocious. His descriptions are repetitive (except when he resorts to the copout of the horror so horrible it cannot be described). His heroes are generally wussies prone to faint dead away at the hint of an oozing tentacle. And I'm not even going to get into his virulent racism and unfortunate choice in cat names. But taken in smaller doses, Providence's favorite misogynist/misanthrope/closet case can be surprisingly effective. Lovecraft revels in "foetors" and "ichors" and "squamous" cosmic vaginas from outer outer outer space. In the 1920s and '30s, he and his cronies imagined a pantheon of other-dimensional monsters that influenced just about every other writer in the genre past and present and that still has comic book evangelist Jack Chick quaking today. This best-of collection includes many of Lovecraft's classics: "The Rats in the Walls," "The Call of Cthulhu," "The Dunwich Horror" and others. It's the only book the casual fan of Lovecraft should ever need. I kept it by my bedside for whenever I was in the mood for his brand of overcooked pulp. Give me Robert E. Howard any other time of the year, but around Halloween, I always get the hankering to make a return trip to the slimy streets of Arkham. <br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="2439163609225968322">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_128682350">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/128682350" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/2224950-kathryn" class="left imgcol" title="Kathryn"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1428875161p2/2224950.jpg" alt="Kathryn" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/128682350?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Nov 07, 2010</a>

      <a href="/user/show/2224950-kathryn" class="user" title="Kathryn">Kathryn</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/36315.The_Best_of_H_P_Lovecraft" title="Paperback 9780345350800" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/2224950-kathryn?shelf=reread-books" class="actionLinkLite">reread-books</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/2224950-kathryn?shelf=2000" class="actionLinkLite">2000</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/2224950-kathryn?shelf=2010" class="actionLinkLite">2010</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer128682350">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2136560600394227207">First Recorded Reading: October 17, 2000<br /><br /><br />Many people acknowledge Edgar Allan Poe (1809 – 1849) as the originator of macabre stories, and Stephen King (1947 – ) as the current holder of that title. But, O my best beloved, there was yet another writer who came between Poe and King, covering the same territory, and that author was H. P. Lovecraft (1890 – 1937). He is one of my personal favorite authors, and I have read this current collection before; but that ‘before’ was before I was doing Book Rev</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2136560600394227207">First Recorded Reading: October 17, 2000<br /><br /><br />Many people acknowledge Edgar Allan Poe (1809 – 1849) as the originator of macabre stories, and Stephen King (1947 – ) as the current holder of that title. But, O my best beloved, there was yet another writer who came between Poe and King, covering the same territory, and that author was H. P. Lovecraft (1890 – 1937). He is one of my personal favorite authors, and I have read this current collection before; but that ‘before’ was before I was doing Book Reviews, so I will now take this opportunity to review this collection of tales, which was my bedtime reading for the past couple of weeks. (And I slept quite well, thank you; and for those not wishing to read further, I loved this book.)<br /><br />This collection of short stories (although some are not very short) tosses us into the Universe of Lovecraft, which has, among other things, several books mentioned which are too horrible to contemplate, and always bring horror into the lives of those who read them (especially the <em>Necronomicon</em>, by the “Mad Arab” Abdul Alhazred), Miskatonic University in Arkham, Massachusetts, on the Miskatonic River, and beings (mostly evil) who pre-date mankind by many ages and who are very dangerous to mess with. In these stories, people keep getting mixed up in weird stuff, and thereby coming to horrible ends. Witchcraft and wizardry are alive and well, and there are scattered degenerated groups all over the Lovecraft universe who worship the “Old Ones”, and wish to call up Cthulhu and other nasty beings.<br /><br />All of this material is described quite floridly; and one has to love a sentence like “It was just past five, with the bloated, fungoid moon sinking in the west, when I staggered into camp – hatless, tattered, features scratched and ensanguined, and without my electric torch” (from “The Shadow Out Of Time”). Indeed, one of the few faults of Lovecraft is that he gives such a huge buildup to some horrible, not-t0-be-imagined climax, so that when the climax finally arrives it has no hope of living up to expectations.<br /><br />Having said all of that, my favorite story in this collection is “The Colour Out of Space”, or Why You Should Not Stick Around When The Meteorite Plows Into Your Farm. I also enjoyed the parts of “The Call of Cthulhu” regarding degraded inhabitants living somewhere in the swamps south of New Orleans. And it should be noted that the main character in “The Haunter of the Dark”, one Robert Blake, is based on Robert Bloch (the later author of <em>Psycho</em>), to whom the story was dedicated, and who was one of Lovecraft’s correspondents. (Bloch also provides the Introduction to this collection.)<br /><br />For those of my readers who have not read any H. P. Lovecraft, this is a dandy collection of stories to start with; and I wish to advise my readers that many items of quality Miskatonic University apparel are available on the Internet, for those who wish to show their school spirit in an appropriate manner.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="2136560600394227207">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/673821432?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Nov 03, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/22410988-robert" class="user" title="Robert">Robert</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/36315.The_Best_of_H_P_Lovecraft" title="Paperback 9780345350800" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer673821432">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7227264777700126553"> When I bought this book at a Waldenbooks when I was all of 10 years old, I thought I knew what horror was. I was a big fan of the all the horror movies of the 1980's and read my mom's Stephen King books. I will admit; the cover is what we drew me in. When I started to read the stories within, I discovered that horror was more then cheap thrills and a girl running away from a nut with an axe. <br /><br /> Lovecraft's stories showed me a world where you would be driven to madness if you tried to be the hero</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText7227264777700126553"> When I bought this book at a Waldenbooks when I was all of 10 years old, I thought I knew what horror was. I was a big fan of the all the horror movies of the 1980's and read my mom's Stephen King books. I will admit; the cover is what we drew me in. When I started to read the stories within, I discovered that horror was more then cheap thrills and a girl running away from a nut with an axe. <br /><br /> Lovecraft's stories showed me a world where you would be driven to madness if you tried to be the hero and stand against something like Cthulhu. No witty one liners from the "heroes" who (temporarily) hold back the return of the Old Ones and no catchphrases from any shoggoth. This was a world where humanity was nothing but an afterthought, something that wallows in mud and slime and is oblivious to the stark coldness of a universe that does not care whether or not you have faith. <br /><br /> These concepts were very new for a kid, and it took well into my later teens until I could fully appreciate Lovecraft. Yet to this day, I cannot think of a better introduction to the existential horror of the Cthulhu Mythos then this book. And the cover art is still cool after all of these years. </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_140649802">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/140649802" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/2064885-jonathan" class="left imgcol" title="Jonathan"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1235438694p2/2064885.jpg" alt="Jonathan" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/140649802?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jan 13, 2011</a>

      <a href="/user/show/2064885-jonathan" class="user" title="Jonathan">Jonathan</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/5054852-the-best-of-h-p-lovecraft" title="ebook 9780345463296" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Sci-fi/horror fans</span></div>

        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/2064885-jonathan?shelf=sci-fi-fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">sci-fi-fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer140649802">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12448796866688722486"> H.P. Lovecraft, in the centuries following his death, has moved steadily from cult author to geek favorite to full-blown pop culture icon. The phenomenon of Lovecraft, and especially the <em>Cthulhu</em> Mythos which he created, has manifested in ways the man himself would never have imagined – this past Christmas my brother gave our two-year-old nephew a stuffed plush toy of the cuddly li’l mind-destroying abomination. So it was, as references to Lovecraft’s works continued to pile up on every geek blo</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText12448796866688722486"> H.P. Lovecraft, in the centuries following his death, has moved steadily from cult author to geek favorite to full-blown pop culture icon. The phenomenon of Lovecraft, and especially the <em>Cthulhu</em> Mythos which he created, has manifested in ways the man himself would never have imagined – this past Christmas my brother gave our two-year-old nephew a stuffed plush toy of the cuddly li’l mind-destroying abomination. So it was, as references to Lovecraft’s works continued to pile up on every geek blog and webcomic, and I began to play games like the outstanding <a rel="nofollow" href="http://www.boardgamegeek.com/boardgame/15987/arkham-horror">
  <em>Arkham Horror</em>
</a>, that I decided it was time to read the stories that inspired it all.<br /><br /> This collection of Lovecraft’s works served as an excellent primer to the man and the Mythos. It leads off with a few shorter tales, early works which aren’t connected with the Mythos proper but are valuable to set the mood of the book as a whole. After this brief introduction, we dive right into nine of the most recognizable Mythos novellas, beginning with Big Daddy, “The Call of Cthulhu”. These longer stories, usually set in or around the fictional town of Arkham, Mass, ubiquitously feature an unsuspecting narrator discovering some hideous secret regarding the existence of the ancient, alien, usually malevolent beings who still lurk in the forgotten reaches of the world. Despite the rather ostentatious claim implicit in the collection’s title, the stories aren’t really all that scary. Lovecraft’s ideas certainly would have been shocking in his own day, nearly a century ago, but since then, alien invaders have been done to death. Lovecraft’s plots, again probably more so to modern readers, are most often fairly transparent; the reader has guessed the horrible secret long before the incredulous narrator can bring himself to utter it. These criticisms are not serious, however.<br /><br /> The main attraction of Lovecraft’s works, now as then, is not the thrill of mystery and horror, not the shock of an unexpected twist, nor even the vast collection of otherworldly beings he concocted, but rather the incredible stylistic sense with which he writes. We know what’s going to happen, we know it’s not really that scary, but that doesn’t stop us from being pulled along by the gripping atmospherics of Lovecraft’s prose. A perfect example is “The Shadow Out of Time,” in which the narrator’s final shocking discovery, long guessed by the reader, is still rendered so convincingly that it induces a shudder. Plain and simply, the man can write, and it shows. At times the excessive gloom can be a bit ridiculous, but stepping away from the stories for a moment should be sufficient cure.<br /><br /> And this is not to say that there aren’t any moments of real shock. The overall path of most of the stories may be somewhat poorly concealed, but there are moments in most that reader won’t see coming. And some of the stories aren’t as transparent: “The Color Out of Space” remained a hellish mystery all the way through, while the final horrible revelation in “The Thing on the Doorstep” really did get to me.<br /><br /> Of all the tales here, the one that stuck with me the most was not one of the long Mythos works, but rather a shorter story, <a rel="nofollow" href="http://www.hplovecraft.com/writings/texts/fiction/sk.asp">"The Silver Key."</a> It borders tangentially on the Mythos proper, and its protagonist, Randolph Carter, appears in at least one other story, but it wasn’t the plot that grasped my attention. Rather, I found myself fascinated by the story’s opening paragraphs, in which Lovecraft builds the philosophical framework in which Carter resides. The author may have intended this brief passage only to set the atmosphere for the tale, but I was so interested by what Lovecraft had to say, intentional or otherwise, about his protagonist’s epistemology that the story itself was rather a letdown. In frank, frighteningly unapologetic terms, Lovecraft has described the classical position of the postmodernist (in itself an amusing irony, but remarkable when one considers he was writing in the late 1920’s). How autobiographical this section is I don’t know, but it seems to me that of all the words of his I’ve read these are the most important to understanding the man’s work.<br /><br /> “When Randolph Carter was thirty he lost the key of the gate of dreams,” our narrative begins, and then we are given a brief summary of Carter’s attempts to replace what he has lost. At every turn, however, Carter finds that whatever facet of our mundane world he examines falls short of the beauty of his half-remembered dream world. First he looks to the scientific world, seeking in the mysteries of the atomic or cosmic realms the wonder he had found in his dreams, but he fails to find it in “things whose laws are known and measurable.” Moreover, he cannot ascribe any real import to them, “as the blind cosmos grinds aimlessly on from nothing to something and from something back to nothing again.” Then he turns to the church, “for thence stretched mystic avenues which seemed to promise escape from life,” but he is quickly repulsed by “the owlish gravity and grotesque claims of solid truth” he finds instead (and heaven forbid that anyone, particularly the adherents of Christianity, should lay any claims to objective truth). Having forsaken religion he turns to the humanists, who seek solidarity in our common experience. These, too, are flawed, however, as “they did not see that good and evil and beauty and ugliness are only ornamental fruits of perspective, whose sole value lies in their linkage to what chance made our fathers think and feel.” Unsatisfied and unimpressed with some six thousand years of human philosophy, Carter resumes his search for the key to his dreams.<br /><br /> What fascinates me so much about this brief introduction to a largely forgettable story is the start, uncompromising language Lovecraft uses to describe the world. The examples I’ve quoted are just a small handful in a sea of scornful denunciation. And the scary thing is that he is spot on in his assessments. Religion, if it does not indeed rest upon some overarching objectivity, is simply worthless at best, duplicitous and malicious at worst. Materialism, whether as objective fact or relativistic interpretation, offers no moral guidance; in fact it would seem to condone lawlessness and anarchy. Humanism, though a noble sentiment, is ultimately empty as it lacks any sort of enforcing authority outside of the human agents it purports to govern.<br /><br /> So is Carter correct to seek refuge in his dreams? Let’s examine more closely his ultimate conclusion. At some point during his search, Carter realizes that, “he had forgotten that all life is only a set of pictures in the brain, among which there is no difference betwixt those born of real things and those born of inward dreamings, and no cause to value the one above the other.” But he does value one over the other, specifically his dream-world. Why? “Calm, lasting beauty comes only in a dream, and this solace the world had thrown away when in its worship of the real it threw away the secrets of childhood and innocence.” Carter seeks only beauty, but, sentiments concerning the eye of the beholder notwithstanding, in abandoning the objective for the aesthetic he has made aesthetics into his standard of objectivity. Thus his relativism collapses in upon itself, as relativism will always do.<br /><br /> “But wait,” the astute reader will cry, “if Carter’s relativism is illogical, how come you said Lovecraft was correct in lambasting the various proponents of objectivism?” Yes, I did say that, because Lovecraft’s arguments are, as far as they go, quite correct. However, I also inserted a caveat, which will lead said astute reader to the actual, correct option (if you need a hint, read any of the other reviews I’ve posted on this site). Suffice it to say that there is objective truth and that we can know it, though not through any effort of our own.<br /><br /> So there you have it: a collection chock full of intriguing sci-fi/horror tales, and one rather inconspicuous but monstrously fascinating philosophical treatise. Even if the latter isn’t quite your cup of tea, this anthology is still an ideal entry point into Lovecraft’s fiction. The presence of so many of his most indelible tales and characters makes sure of that. If you’re into sci-fi/horror, and want to see how we got from Edgar Allan Poe to Neil Gaiman, pick up this collection.<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="12448796866688722486">...more</a>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_477057555">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/477057555" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/12147733-david-stephens" class="left imgcol" title="David Stephens"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1374984367p2/12147733.jpg" alt="David Stephens" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/477057555?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Dec 13, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/12147733-david-stephens" class="user" title="David Stephens">David Stephens</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/36315.The_Best_of_H_P_Lovecraft" title="Paperback 9780345350800" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/12147733-david-stephens?shelf=horror" class="actionLinkLite">horror</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer477057555">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10227904451544720539">One would think after all the bizarre supernatural events that occur in Lovecraft's fictional New England, a larger swath of people around the world would take notice and attempt to do something about them. In some cases, portals to other dimensions or remnants of ancient beings are even located squarely within large cities and, yet, remain undisclosed to the masses. This is, of course, mostly due to two things: because some citizens have a vague knowledge of the evil lurking nearby and wisely c</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10227904451544720539">One would think after all the bizarre supernatural events that occur in Lovecraft's fictional New England, a larger swath of people around the world would take notice and attempt to do something about them. In some cases, portals to other dimensions or remnants of ancient beings are even located squarely within large cities and, yet, remain undisclosed to the masses. This is, of course, mostly due to two things: because some citizens have a vague knowledge of the evil lurking nearby and wisely choose to stay away from it and because many others display a flippancy toward superstitions, regarding them as wild tales.<br /><br />For the true believers, though, Lovecraft's stories could certainly act as a warning for anyone planning on unearthing secrets of the haunted backwoods or the sinister urban areas of New England. The stories take readers past the traditional New England of the 1920s and 30s to the nightmarish underworlds that lie beneath. A passage from "The Shadow Over Innsmouth" perfectly illustrates the dark eastern landscape: "Collapsing huddles of gambrel roofs formed a jagged and fantastic skyline, above which rose the ghoulish, decapitated steeple of an ancient church." In Lovecraft's world, not even the steeples can keep their heads on.<br /><br />The stories typically deal with a protagonist who has stumbled upon some unknown species of ancient beings ("cosmic horror") and, therefore, acquires knowledge not fit for the human mind. This knowledge then causes the protagonist to lose control of his sanity or lead a life of paranoia as unknown assailants pursue him. There are stories that fall outside of this formula as well—like "In the Vault," one of my personal favorites—but even within this framework, Lovecraft manages to keep each story fresh and exciting. Revelations are doled out at the right pace, Lovecraft-invented folklore is mixed with true accounts of obscure occultism, and various monsters are described in such a way that gives the basic structure of their forms but never quite completes the picture, leaving readers with only vague impressions of the monstrosities that abound.<br /><br />One of the big questions for me about Lovecraft's writing is whether he gives away too much information or not. Unfortunately, I feel he often does. There are stories like "The Call of Chtulu" and "The Shadow Out of Time" that almost seem to be informational texts about the Chtulu mythos rather than traditional tales, and here the excess of information might work well. However, in other cases, most notably "The Thing on the Doorstep," stories would have benefitted from revealing far less. It is leaving certain things unknown—as Lovecraft was well aware—that creates real terror. Therefore, I find myself somewhat disappointed that more fear isn't created, but even that doesn't diminish the pleasure I derive from inhabiting the Lovecraft universe and not wanting to leave it.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="10227904451544720539">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_105854837">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/105854837" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/62208-katey" class="left imgcol" title="Katey"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1281148810p2/62208.jpg" alt="Katey" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/105854837?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jul 26, 2011</a>

      <a href="/user/show/62208-katey" class="user" title="Katey">Katey</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok">it was ok</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/62208-katey?shelf=fiction" class="actionLinkLite">fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/62208-katey?shelf=short-stories" class="actionLinkLite">short-stories</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/62208-katey?shelf=partially-read" class="actionLinkLite">partially-read</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/62208-katey?shelf=abandon-all-hope" class="actionLinkLite">abandon-all-hope</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer105854837">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13127025226101636772">Perhaps it is that I really, <em>really</em> dislike the horror genre, or that I had heard so much fuss over Lovecraft and from other cool sources heard about the Cthulhu mythos and others, that I was so absolutely certain I'd adore Lovecraft. Wrong. And I tried. I can see a lot of things that others see as good, but personally, I felt all the stories were way too similar, written in the same voice. And I didn't need a sophmoric foreword to explain the obvious themes in his writing; the mild societal rac</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText13127025226101636772">Perhaps it is that I really, <em>really</em> dislike the horror genre, or that I had heard so much fuss over Lovecraft and from other cool sources heard about the Cthulhu mythos and others, that I was so absolutely certain I'd adore Lovecraft. Wrong. And I tried. I can see a lot of things that others see as good, but personally, I felt all the stories were way too similar, written in the same voice. And I didn't need a sophmoric foreword to explain the obvious themes in his writing; the mild societal racism. The cats. The Anglophiliac spelling (I quite liked that, actually).<br /><br />After a few the-same-but-not stories, I decided to read another book, then read one Lovecraft short story. And I HATE reading books so agnonizingly slow like that. But I thought Lovecraft would be worth it in the long run. Wrong. I ended up skipping the last two stories, as I just couldn't take it anymore. I want to be done and away with it, secure in my deeper understanding of further Lovecraftian references I encounter in the future.<br /><br />There was just too much drama. It almost made it comedic. It's clear that Lovecraft needed to get laid more. A LOT more, if he did at all. There was something in his writing that was too clipped and Puritanical, even through the uneccessary drama. It seemed like he needed a vicarious infusion of excitement, even if the story didn't.<br /><br />I don't begrudge anyone for liking Lovecraft. If anything, I hold it against myself that I don't. "It's okay" sums it up the best. Rating systems make it so easy to compartmentalize opinions!<br /><br />Oh, and this published edition is riddled with typos. I'm sure that left a sour taste in my mouth.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="13127025226101636772">...more</a>

          </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_4442043">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/4442043" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/272983-l-s" class="left imgcol" title="L S"><img src="http://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" alt="L S" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/4442043?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Aug 12, 2007</a>

      <a href="/user/show/272983-l-s" class="user" title="L S">L S</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/36315.The_Best_of_H_P_Lovecraft" title="Paperback 9780345350800" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer4442043">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13373213781864161695">I've read the Lovecraft stories in this volume and others a bunch of times. I caution the reader, though, that every time I have recommended Lovecraft to an adult, the adult has scoffed at him as overblown and baroque. I don't disagree with this sentiment but I find that Lovecraft's self-conscious ornamentation adds rather than detracts from the world he creates. For historical reasons, Lovecraft is a must-read. He is generally credited as being the forerunner of modern horror and one of the rea</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText13373213781864161695">I've read the Lovecraft stories in this volume and others a bunch of times. I caution the reader, though, that every time I have recommended Lovecraft to an adult, the adult has scoffed at him as overblown and baroque. I don't disagree with this sentiment but I find that Lovecraft's self-conscious ornamentation adds rather than detracts from the world he creates. For historical reasons, Lovecraft is a must-read. He is generally credited as being the forerunner of modern horror and one of the reasons readers have trouble with him is that he invented themes that have now become commonplace in horror film and literature. <br /><br />Nonetheless, the Chthulu mythos that he created remains fascinating and different to me. In particular, I enjoy his clever sleight of literary hand to heighten terror by always keeping the terrible just out sight, to be imagined in the periphery, creeping up behind you on your way back from the toilet at 3:30 in the morning. I also delight in the inherent evanescence of revelation in his literary world; a commonplace in Lovecraft is that the more a protagonist discovers regarding reality, the more madness sets in and effaces the character's ability to articulate or even rationally understand the truths revealed to him. This move in the horror game is just genius and is reflective of Lovecraft's clever use of the obfuscation to heighten horror. <br /><br />His dream world is just as interesting, and in this case often pleasant rather than terrible. Lovecraft dips into the childhood belief that magic exists under the surface of technology and reason. <br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="13373213781864161695">...more</a>

          </span>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown">
    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_150612927">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/150612927" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/384892-east-bay-j" class="left imgcol" title="East Bay J"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1392658934p2/384892.jpg" alt="East Bay J" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/150612927?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jan 14, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/384892-east-bay-j" class="user" title="East Bay J">East Bay J</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/384892-east-bay-j?shelf=short-story-collections" class="actionLinkLite">short-story-collections</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer150612927">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17245337120738198346">It hadn’t been a real long while since I last read Lovecraft but I attacked <em>Bloodcurdling Tales</em> with relish. I prefer Lovecraft’s weird tales to his dream quest stuff and that’s what this one’s all about. This book is the perfect introduction to Lovecraft for the newcomer and a great way to revisit his work for the longtime fan.<br /><br />Many of the classics are here including some of my favorites like “Pickman’s Model,” “Dreams In The Witch House” and “In The Vault.” “The Call Of Cthulhu,” and other stor</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText17245337120738198346">It hadn’t been a real long while since I last read Lovecraft but I attacked <em>Bloodcurdling Tales</em> with relish. I prefer Lovecraft’s weird tales to his dream quest stuff and that’s what this one’s all about. This book is the perfect introduction to Lovecraft for the newcomer and a great way to revisit his work for the longtime fan.<br /><br />Many of the classics are here including some of my favorites like “Pickman’s Model,” “Dreams In The Witch House” and “In The Vault.” “The Call Of Cthulhu,” and other stories that fell into what was later called the “Cthulhu Mythos” are in abundance as well as a few that are just good old horror stories. <br /><br />This Del Rey volume is one of the first Lovecraft collections I ever read. I don’t know how old I was at the time. I vividly recall the stories having a fairly profound effect on me, or the writing, or the world Lovercraft’s character’s existed in, or something. It resonated pretty heavily in my psyche, for whatever reason, for better or for worse.<br /><br />What I don’t recall was what my reaction was to Lovecraft’s racism. I don’t remember thinking anything about it. Reading it now, it must have been obvious. I mean, first story, “Rats In The Walls,” bang, there it is. The protagonist’s cat’s name is a racial slur, this cat named after Lovercraft’s own beloved cat. I know I was aware of racism and bigotry and that I must have had some kind of reaction to this at the time but I can’t remember.<br /><br />In recent memory, I’ve noticed the racism in the writing of Robert E. Howard, Edgar Rice Burroughs, H. Rider Haggard and Rudyard Kipling. Few of these authors’ fans seem eager to engage in a dialogue about the issue, let alone comment on it. Probably the most thorough and accurate analysis I’ve read is this one by Bruce Lord:<br /><br /><a rel="nofollow" href="http://www.contrasoma.com/writing/lovecraft.html" target="_blank">http://www.contrasoma.com/writing/lov...</a><br /><br />In addition to Lovecraft's racism, this article addresses his fear of sex and women, so it’s pretty interesting, if fairly dry. There is also a great Revolution Science Fiction water cooler on Lovecraft and racism here: <br /><br /><a rel="nofollow" href="http://www.revolutionsf.com/article.php?id=5394" target="_blank">http://www.revolutionsf.com/article.p...</a><br /><br />The best quote I gleaned from that was:<br /><br />“Does the text of Lovecraft’s stories contain racism and is it of the type and/or frequency that would make me hesitate to recommend it to friends, or to hand a Lovecraft anthology to teenagers without also including a civics lesson? If so, that’s damned important stuff to talk about.<br /><br />Beyond that, you’re just wagging your finger at a dead guy.” -Jason Myers<br /><br />And enough on that topic.<br /><br />Because I hadn’t read so many of these stories in such a long time, I did pick up on things I’m sure I didn’t as a kid. The fact that Pluto had just been discovered lead the science and astronomy loving Lovecraft to incorporate the planet as Yuggoth, way station to ancient evil. I have such a poor grasp on the chronology of events, so it’s interesting to see how a scientific discovery of Lovercraft’s time entered his fiction. I loved the “handy three-in-one device including a screw-driver which I kept on my key-ring” from “The Shadow Over Innsmouth.” I didn’t even know they HAD those in the 20’s!<br /><br />Also, devil worship! I grew up with the whole devil worship thing going on, talk show hosts talking about which bands were satanic, KISS supposedly standing for Knights In Satan’s Service, rumors of local locations where Satanic rituals were rumored to take place. Reading Lovecraft’s descriptions of depraved devil worshippers got me wondering, “When did it all begin?” It seems to have existed at least as far back as the 18th century, though the earliest references may have come from Christians accusing pagans of worshipping the devil during their ancestral rites. I like to think that somebody, one day, said, “Yeah, okay, Satan, right, yep, I worship him,” y’know, that first drop, the ripples of which continue to disturb modern society to this day.<br /><br />And what about this word, “cyclopean?” What does it really mean? Lovecraft uses it to mean “enormous” or something to that effect. Large beyond human comprehension. I thought the word was a Lovercraft invention but, as it turns out, it’s a real word! According to Wikipedia, “The term comes from the belief of classical Greeks that only the mythical Cyclopes had the strength to move the enormous boulders that made up the walls of Mycenae and Tiryns. Pliny’s <em>Natural History</em> reported the tradition attributed to Aristotle, that the Cyclopes were the inventors of masonry towers, giving rise to the designation Cyclopean."<br /><br />Something that one can glean and appreciate from these stories is the existence and effect of the “Lovecraft Circle,” a select group of pulp writers who became pen pals and borrowed ideas from one another. Robert E. Howard gets a nod in “The Shadow Out Of Time” when Lovecraft sites “Crom-Ya, a Cimmerian chieftan of 15,000 B.C.” Clark Ashton Smith becomes the dark sorcerer, Klarkash-Ton, in “The Whisperer In Darkness.” Robert Bloch gets a shout in the form of protagonist, Robert Blake in “The Haunter Of The Dark,” which Lovecraft dedicated to him. It was from Reading Howard and Lovecraft that I learned of the “Lovecraft Circle” and began to dele into the writing of the other authors. I think that’s cool, and fun and I like it.<br /><br />I read another Del Rey collection, <em>Dreams Of Terror And Death</em>, not so long ago. That was published thirteen years after <em>Bloodcurdling Tales</em> but I find it questionable that “Pickman’s Model,” “The Silver Key” and “Dreams In The Witch House” appear in both collections. Put them in one or the other, select a few more stories and be done with it. Is that not the obvious choice? It seems Del Rey has done it again with their Robert E. Howard collections. It’s no egregious crime but it is a shame other stories were not included instead of repeats.<br /><br />Robert Bloch’s introduction is excellent. There’s a great quote that provides and interesting take on modern horror writers and film makers:<br /><br />“The source of a writer’s personal fantasies is to a great extent colored by what he reads or views during his impressionable early years. Today’s fictioneers and filmmakers often come late to an acquaintance with literary traditions. Their youthful influences were often the science fiction films and comic books of the fifties and sixties. As a result, a great deal of what they present as horror relies on crude and explicit violence. Dwelling on gruesome detail in print and utilizing special effects for shock on the screen, they opt for the easy out of evoking nausea and revulsion instead of genuine fear. Even their lighter efforts, involving the renascence of “super-heroes,” betray an intellectual dependency on the comics of their childhood. As such, their efforts find greater favor with juvenile audiences of today.”<br /><br />You change a few words and that becomes what I see as a fairly accurate indictment of modern music, as well, never mind this was written around 1982.<br /><br />If you wanna’ read some Lovecraft, you can’t go wrong with <em>Bloodcurdling Tales</em>. Highly recommended.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="17245337120738198346">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer9981270573369662206">The thing I always found odd about Robert E. Howard was how, despite his own racism, his work seemed ambivalent on the matter. I remember one Conan st</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText9981270573369662206">The thing I always found odd about Robert E. Howard was how, despite his own racism, his work seemed ambivalent on the matter. I remember one Conan story in which a black prince attempts to execute the titluar hero because Conan had been hired to (successfully) kill the prince's brother; the prince disgustedly states that only a "white dog" would be so inhuman as to kill for money. Conan only survives because a monster kills the prince, who otherwise would have succeeded in killing the protagonist. <br /><br />By contrast, Lovecraft's depiction of race seems far more consistent with his actual views. Which were...not positive.</span>

  <span style="display:none" id="freeText17022154944053389797">That's an excellent point. Perhaps Howard wasn't concerned enough about it to let it creep into his writing. Where Howard's black characters were often as heroic as anyone else in his tales, Lovecraft seemed intent on making some kind of statement regarding his racial prejudice. For the most part, anyone in Lovecraft's stories who wasn't of English heritage was considered inferior.</span>

    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked &amp;&amp; exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }

      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }

    }

P. Lovecraft (1890-1937), a Providence author considered by many to be the finest horror story writer of the twentieth century. Lovecraft\'s tales are a blend of fantasy, science fiction, and horror, with the&lt;\/span&gt;\n  &lt;span id=\"freeText2766744900626823759\" style=\"display:none\"&gt;The Cthulhu Mythos was first created by H. P. Lovecraft (1890-1937), a Providence author considered by many to be the finest horror story writer of the twentieth century. Lovecraft\'s tales are a blend of fantasy, science fiction, and horror, with the latter being especially prominent. Many of his tales describe a pantheon of powerful beings known as the Great Old Ones.Since Lovecraft\'s time the Cthulhu Mythos has grown exponentially, until it has become increasingly difficult to keep track of, even for devoted fans. Many writers have contributed to it, including Robert E. Howard, Robert Bloch, Brian Lumley, and Stephen King. This book is the first major attempt in many years to provide a comprehensive guide to H. P. Lovecraft\'s Cthulhu Mythos.\n\nThis second edition of Encyclopedia Cthulhiana has been extensively revised and contains over a hundred and fifty additional pages and scores of new entries. New features include thumbnail illustrations of the most important signs and symbols and a timeline of the Cthulhu Mythos spanning billions of years. Many entries have been revised to reflect our latest understanding of the Mythos, and the infamous Necronomicon appendix has been greatly expanded. Also present for the first time is &amp;quot;A Brief History of the Cthulhu Mythos,&amp;quot; which examines the evolution of the genre.&lt;\/span&gt;\n  &lt;a data-text-id=\"2766744900626823759\" For those who already know these tales, the poignant introduction is a window on the creator\'s mind. Reflecting back after 14 years, Barker writes:  I look at these pieces and I don\'t think the man who wrote them is alive in me anymore.... We are all our own graveyards I believe; we squat amongst the tombs of the people we were. If we\'re healthy, every day is a celebration, a Day of the Dead, in which we give thanks for the lives that we lived; and if we are neurotic we brood and mourn and wish that the past was still present. Reading these stories over, I feel a little of both. Some of the simple energies that made these words flow through my pen--that made the phrases felicitous and the ideas sing--have gone. I lost their maker a long time ago.  These enthusiastic tales are not ashamed of visceral horror, of blood splashing freely across the page: &amp;quot;The Midnight Meat Train,&amp;quot; a grisly subway tale that surprises you with one twist after another; &amp;quot;The Yattering and Jack,&amp;quot; about a hilarious demon who possesses a Christmas turkey; &amp;quot;In the Hills, the Cities,&amp;quot; an unusual example of an original horror premise; &amp;quot;Dread,&amp;quot; a harrowing non-supernatural tale about being forced to realize your worst nightmare; &amp;quot;Jacqueline Ess: Her Will and Testament,&amp;quot; about a woman who kills men with her mind. Some of the tales are more successful than others, but all are distinguished by strikingly beautiful images of evil and destruction. No horror library is complete without them. --Fiona Webster&lt;\/span&gt;\n  &lt;a data-text-id=\"3554739404076854804\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent($(this));; return Joshi is the recognized authority on all things Lovecraftian, and is famous for his restorations of Lovecraft\'s original works.     <div class=" clearFloats bigBox" id="aboutAuthor"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/9494.H_P_Lovecraft">About H.P. Lovecraft</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
      <a href="/author/show/9494.H_P_Lovecraft"><img alt="9494" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/authors/1299165714p2/9494.jpg" class="right" /></a>
    <div class="readable">
      
<span id="freeTextContainer11011675977174731871">Howard Phillips Lovecraft, of Providence, Rhode Island, was an American author of horror, fantasy and science fiction.<br /><br />Lovecraft's major inspiration and invention was cosmic horror: life is incomprehensible to human minds and the universe is fundamentally alien. Those who genuinely reason, like his protagonists, gamble with sanity. Lovecraft has developed a cult following for his Cthulhu Mythos, a</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText11011675977174731871">Howard Phillips Lovecraft, of Providence, Rhode Island, was an American author of horror, fantasy and science fiction.<br /><br />Lovecraft's major inspiration and invention was cosmic horror: life is incomprehensible to human minds and the universe is fundamentally alien. Those who genuinely reason, like his protagonists, gamble with sanity. Lovecraft has developed a cult following for his Cthulhu Mythos, a series of loosely interconnected fictions featuring a pantheon of human-nullifying entities, as well as the Necronomicon, a fictional grimoire of magical rites and forbidden lore. His works were deeply pessimistic and cynical, challenging the values of the Enlightenment, Romanticism and Christianity. Lovecraft's protagonists usually achieve the mirror-opposite of traditional gnosis and mysticism by momentarily glimpsing the horror of ultimate reality.<br /><br />Although Lovecraft's readership was limited during his life, his reputation has grown over the decades. He is now commonly regarded as one of the most influential horror writers of the 20th Century, exerting widespread and indirect influence, and frequently compared to Edgar Allan Poe.<br /><br />— <a rel="nofollow" href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/H._P._Lovecraft">Wikipedia</a></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="11011675977174731871">...more</a>

    </div>
    <a href="/author/show/9494.H_P_Lovecraft" class="actionLink moreLink">More about H.P. Lovecraft...</a>
